</td>

<p>The <b>House Sparrow</b> (<i>Passer domesticus</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">bird</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sparrow" title="Sparrow">sparrow</a> family Passeridae, found in most parts of the world. A small bird, it has a typical length of 16&#160;cm (6.3&#160;in) and a weight of 24–39.5 g (0.85–1.39 oz). Females and young birds are coloured pale brown and grey, and males have brighter black, white, and brown markings. One of about 25&#160;<a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Passer" title="Passer">Passer</a></i>, the House Sparrow occurs naturally in most of Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_region" title="Mediterranean region" class="mw-redirect">Mediterranean region</a>, and much of Asia. Its intentional or accidental <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introductions</a> to many regions, including parts of Australia, Africa, and the Americas, make it the most widely distributed wild bird.</p>
<p>The House Sparrow is strongly associated with human habitations, and can live in urban or rural settings. Though found in widely varied habitats and climates, it typically avoids extensive <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grasslands</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a> away from human development. It feeds mostly on the seeds of grains and weeds, but it is an opportunistic eater and commonly eats insects and many other foods. Its predators include <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">domestic cats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a>, and many other predatory birds and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>.</p>
<p>Because of its numbers, ubiquity and association with human settlements, the sparrow is culturally prominent. It is extensively, and usually unsuccessfully, persecuted as an agricultural pest, but it has also often been kept as a pet as well as being a food item and a symbol of lust and sexual potency, as well as of commonness and vulgarity. Though it is widespread and abundant, its numbers have declined in some areas. The animal's <a href="/wiki/Conservation_status" title="Conservation status">conservation status</a> is listed as <a href="/wiki/Least_Concern" title="Least Concern">Least Concern</a> on the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<p>The House Sparrow is a compact bird,<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> typically about 16&#160;cm (6.3&#160;in) long, ranging from 14–18 cm (5.5–7.1 in).<sup id="cite_ref-Summers116.E2.80.93117_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers116.E2.80.93117-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> It has a large rounded head, and a stout bill with a <a href="/wiki/Culmen_(bird)" title="Culmen (bird)" class="mw-redirect">culmen</a> length of 1.1 to 1.5 cm (0.43 to 0.59 in). It has a short tail, 5.2 to 6.5 cm (2.0 to 2.6 in) long. The <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> is 6.7 to 8.9 cm (2.6 to 3.5 in), and the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> is 1.6 to 2.5 cm (0.63 to 0.98 in).<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> In weight, the House Sparrow ranges from 24–39.5 g (0.85–1.39 oz). Weight varies by sex, with females usually smaller than males. The median weight on the European continent for both sexes is about 30&#160;g (1.1&#160;oz), and in more southerly subspecies is around 26 grams (0.92&#160;oz). Younger birds are smaller, males are larger during the winter, and females are larger during the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers118.E2.80.93121_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers118.E2.80.93121-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Between and within <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>, there is further variation based on latitude, altitude, climate, and other environmental factors, under biological principles such as <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_rule" title="Bergmann's rule">Bergmann's rule</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers118.E2.80.93121_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers118.E2.80.93121-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-evo-III_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-evo-III-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Plumage">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Plumage">Plumage</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> of the House Sparrow is mostly different shades of grey and brown. The sexes <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">differ</a>: the female is mostly <a href="/wiki/Buff_(colour)" title="Buff (colour)">buff</a>, and the male has bolder markings and a reddish back.<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> The male has a dark grey <a href="/wiki/Crown_(anatomy)" title="Crown (anatomy)">crown</a> from the top of its bill to its back, and chestnut brown on the sides of its head. It has black around its bill, on its throat, and on the spaces between its bill and eyes (<a href="/wiki/Lore_(anatomy)" title="Lore (anatomy)">lores</a>). It has a small white stripe between the lores and crown and small white spots immediately behind the eyes (postoculars), with black patches below and above them. The underparts are pale grey or white, as are the cheeks, ear <a href="/wiki/Covert_(feather)" title="Covert (feather)" class="mw-redirect">coverts</a>, and stripes at the base of the head. The upper back and mantle are a warm brown, with broad black streaks, while the lower back, rump and uppertail coverts are greyish-brown.<sup id="cite_ref-Clement444_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clement444-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The male is duller in fresh non-breeding plumage, with whitish tips on many feathers. Wear and preening expose many of the bright brown and black markings, including most of the black throat and chest patch, called the "bib" or "badge".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> The badge is variable in width and general size, and some scientists have suggested that patches signal social status or fitness, a hypothesis which has led to a "veritable 'cottage industry'" of studies, which have only conclusively shown that patches increase in size with age.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The female has no black markings or grey crown. Its upperparts and head are brown with darker streaks around the mantle and a distinct pale supercilium. Its underparts are pale grey-brown. The juvenile is similar to the female adult but deeper brown below and paler above. Juvenile males tend to have darker throats and white postoculars, but these features do not reliably match sex.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers116.E2.80.93117_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers116.E2.80.93117-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clement444_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clement444-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Voice">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Voice">Voice</span></h3>

<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em;"><span style="font-size:smaller;"><i>Problems listening to this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>

<p>Most House Sparrow <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization" title="Bird vocalization">vocalisations</a> are variations on its short and incessant chirping call. Transcribed as <i>chirrup</i>, <i>tschilp</i>, or <i>philip</i>, this note is made as a contact call by flocking or resting birds, or by males to proclaim nest ownership and invite pairing. In the breeding season the male gives this call repetitively, with emphasis and speed but not much rhythm, forming what is described either as a song or an "ecstatic call", similar to a song.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Young birds also give a true song, especially in captivity, a warbling similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/European_Greenfinch" title="European Greenfinch">European Greenfinch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Aggressive males give a trilled version of their call, transcribed as "chur-<i>chur-r-r-it-it-it-it</i>". This call is also used by females in the breeding season, to establish dominance over males while displacing them to feed young or incubate eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-NN30.E2.80.931_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NN30.E2.80.931-15"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> House Sparrows give a nasal alarm call, the basic sound of which is transcribed as <i>quer</i>, and a shrill <i>chree</i> call in great distress.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Another vocalisation is the "appeasement call", a soft <i>quee</i> given to inhibit aggression, usually given between birds of a mated pair.<sup id="cite_ref-NN30.E2.80.931_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NN30.E2.80.931-15"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> These vocalisations are not unique to the House Sparrow, but are shared, with small variations, by all sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Variation">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Variation">Variation</span></h3>
<p>There is some variation in the twelve subspecies of House Sparrow, which are divided into two groups, the <a href="/wiki/Indomalaya_ecozone" title="Indomalaya ecozone">Oriental</a> <i>indicus</i> group, and the <a href="/wiki/Palearctic_ecozone" title="Palearctic ecozone">Palaearctic</a> <i>domesticus</i> group. Birds of the <i>domesticus</i> group have grey cheeks, while <i>indicus</i> group birds have white cheeks as well as bright colouration on the crown, a smaller bill, and a longer black bib.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The subspecies <i>Passer domesticus tingitanus</i> differs little from the nominate subspecies, except in the worn breeding plumage of the male, in which the head is speckled with black and underparts are paler.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers117_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers117-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>P. d. balearoibericus</i> is slightly paler than the nominate but darker than <i>P. d. bibilicus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>P. d. bibilicus</i> is paler than most subspecies, but has the grey cheeks of <i>domesticus</i> group birds. The similar <i>P. d. persicus</i> is paler and smaller, and <i>P. d. niloticus</i> is nearly identical but smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers117_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers117-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Of the less widespread <i>indicus</i> group subspecies, <i>P. d. hyrcanus</i> is larger than <i>P. d. indicus</i>, <i>P. d. hufufae</i> is paler, <i>P. d. bactrianus</i> is larger and paler, and <i>P. d. parkini</i> is larger and darker with more black on the breast than any other subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers117_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers117-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Identification">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Identification">Identification</span></h3>
<p>The House Sparrow can be confused with a number of other seed-eating birds, especially its relatives in the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i>Passer</i>. Many of these relatives are smaller, with an appearance that is neater or "cuter", as with the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea_Sparrow" title="Dead Sea Sparrow">Dead Sea Sparrow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> The dull-coloured female can often not be distinguished from other females, and is nearly identical to the those of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Sparrow" title="Spanish Sparrow">Spanish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_Sparrow" title="Italian Sparrow">Italian Sparrows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clement444_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clement444-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Tree_Sparrow" title="Eurasian Tree Sparrow">Eurasian Tree Sparrow</a> is smaller and more slender with a chestnut crown and a black patch on each cheek.<sup id="cite_ref-Clement463_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clement463-24"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> The male Spanish Sparrow and Italian Sparrow are distinguished by their chestnut crowns. The <a href="/wiki/Sind_Sparrow" title="Sind Sparrow">Sind Sparrow</a> is very similar but smaller, with less black on the male's throat and a distinct pale supercilium on the female.<sup id="cite_ref-Clement444_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clement444-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Systematics">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Systematics">Systematics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p>The House Sparrow was among the first animals to be given a <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">scientific name</a> in the modern system of <a href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">biological classification</a>, since it was described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a>, in the 1758 <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>. It was described from a <a href="/wiki/Type_specimen" title="Type specimen" class="mw-redirect">type specimen</a> collected in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, with the name <i>Fringilla domestica</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers121.E2.80.93122_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers121.E2.80.93122-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Later the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> name <i><a href="/wiki/Fringilla" title="Fringilla">Fringilla</a></i> came to be used only for the <a href="/wiki/Chaffinch" title="Chaffinch" class="mw-redirect">Chaffinch</a> and its relatives, and the House Sparrow has usually been placed in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Passer" title="Passer">Passer</a></i> created by French zoologist <a href="/wiki/Mathurin_Jacques_Brisson" title="Mathurin Jacques Brisson">Mathurin Jacques Brisson</a> in 1760.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers114.E2.80.93115_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers114.E2.80.93115-27"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The bird's scientific name and its usual English name have the same meaning. The <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>passer</i>, like the English word "sparrow", is a term for small active birds, coming from a root word referring to speed.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The Latin word <i>domesticus</i> means "belonging to the house", like the common name a reference to its association with humans.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> The House Sparrow is also called by a number of alternative English names, including <i>English Sparrow</i>, chiefly in North America;<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> and <i>Indian Sparrow</i> or <i>Indian House Sparrow</i>, for the birds of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Dialectal names include <i>sparr</i>, <i>sparrer</i>, <i>spadger</i>, <i>spadgick</i>, and <i>philip</i>, mainly in southern England; <i>spug</i> and <i>spuggy</i>, mainly in northern England; <i>spur</i> and <i>sprig</i>, mainly in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Provincial_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Provincial-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> and <i>spatzie</i> or <i>spotsie</i>, from the German <i>Spatz</i>, in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h3>
<p>The genus <i>Passer</i> contains about 25 species, depending on the authority, 26 according to the <i><a href="/wiki/Handbook_of_the_Birds_of_the_World" title="Handbook of the Birds of the World">Handbook of the Birds of the World</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Most <i>Passer</i> species are dull-coloured birds with short square tails and stubby conical beaks, between 11 and 18 centimetres (4.3 and 7.1 in) long.<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">Mitochondrial DNA</a> suggest that <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a> in the genus occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> and earlier, while other evidence suggests speciation occurred 25,000 to 15,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Within <i>Passer</i>, the House Sparrow is part of the "<a href="/wiki/Palaearctic" title="Palaearctic" class="mw-redirect">Palaearctic</a> black-bibbed sparrows" group and a close relative of the Mediterranean "willow sparrows".<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The taxonomy of the House Sparrow and its Mediterranean relatives is highly complicated. The common type of "willow sparrow" is the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Sparrow" title="Spanish Sparrow">Spanish Sparrow</a>, which resembles the House Sparrow in many respects.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> It frequently prefers wetter habitats than the House Sparrow, and it is often colonial and nomadic.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> In most of the Mediterranean, one or both species occur, with some degree of <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridisation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> In North Africa, the two species hybridise extensively, forming highly variable mixed populations with a full range of characters from pure House Sparrows to pure Spanish Sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93127_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93127-45"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-T.C3.B6pfer_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T.C3.B6pfer-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In much of Italy there is a form apparently intermediate between the House and Spanish Sparrows, known as the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Sparrow" title="Italian Sparrow">Italian Sparrow</a>. It resembles a hybrid between the two species, and is in other respects intermediate. Its specific status and origin are the subject of much debate.<sup id="cite_ref-T.C3.B6pfer_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T.C3.B6pfer-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, the Italian Sparrow intergrades over a roughly 20 kilometres (12&#160;mi) strip with the House Sparrow,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> but to the south it intergrades over the southern half of Italy and some Mediterranean islands with the Spanish Sparrow.<sup id="cite_ref-T.C3.B6pfer_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T.C3.B6pfer-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> On the Mediterranean islands of <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gozo" title="Gozo">Gozo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karpathos" title="Karpathos">Karpathos</a>, there are other apparently intermediate birds of unknown status.<sup id="cite_ref-T.C3.B6pfer_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T.C3.B6pfer-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow_(Passer_domesticus)-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg/220px-House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg/330px-House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg/440px-House_Sparrow_%28Passer_domesticus%29-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow_(Passer_domesticus)-_Male_in_Kolkata_I_IMG_5904.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A male of <i>P. d. indicus</i> in Kolkata, India</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg/220px-Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg/330px-Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg/440px-Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-Istanbul-8b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A male of <i>P. d. balearoibericus</i> in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A large number of subspecies have been named, of which twelve were recognised in the <i>Handbook of the Birds of the World</i>. These subspecies are divided into two groups, the Palaearctic <i>domesticus</i> group, and the Oriental <i>indicus</i> group.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Several Middle Eastern subspecies, including <i>Passer domesticus biblicus</i>, are sometimes considered a third, intermediate group. The subspecies <i>P. d. indicus</i> was described as a species, and was considered to be distinct by many ornithologists during the nineteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Migratory birds of the subspecies <i>P. d. bactrianus</i> in the <i>indicus</i> group were recorded overlapping with <i>P. d. domesticus</i> birds without hybridising in the 1970s, so the Soviet scientists Edward I. Gavrilov and M. N. Korelov proposed the separation of the <i>indicus</i> group as a separate species.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers114.E2.80.93115_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers114.E2.80.93115-27"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> However, <i>indicus</i>-group and <i>domesticus</i>-group birds <a href="/wiki/Intergradation" title="Intergradation">intergrade</a> in a large part of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, so this split is rarely recognised.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In North America, House Sparrow populations are more differentiated than those in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-evo-III_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-evo-III-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> This variation follows predictable patterns, with birds at higher latitudes being larger and those in arid areas being paler.<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oberholser_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberholser-53"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> However, it is not clear how much this is caused by evolution or by environment.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Similar observations have been made in New Zealand,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> and in South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> The introduced House Sparrow populations may be distinct enough to merit subspecies status, especially in North America and southern Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> and American ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Harry_Church_Oberholser" title="Harry Church Oberholser">Harry Church Oberholser</a> even gave the subspecies name <i>plecticus</i> to the paler birds of western North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Oberholser_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberholser-53"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt><i>domesticus</i> group</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>P. d. domesticus</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Nominate_subspecies" title="Nominate subspecies" class="mw-redirect">nominate subspecies</a>, is found in most of Europe, across northern Asia to <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" title="Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect">Kamchatka</a>. It is the most widely introduced subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers121.E2.80.93122_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers121.E2.80.93122-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. balearoibericus</i> von Jordans, 1923, described from <a href="/wiki/Majorca" title="Majorca">Majorca</a>, is found in the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a>, southern France, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. tingitanus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Victor_Loche" title="Victor Loche">Loche</a>, 1867), described from <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, is found in the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ajdabiya" title="Ajdabiya">Ajdabiya</a> in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> to <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9ni_Abb%C3%A8s" title="Béni Abbès">Béni Abbès</a> in Algeria, and to <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>'s Atlantic coast. It hybridises extensively with the Spanish Sparrow, especially in the eastern part of its range.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. niloticus</i> Nicoll and Bonhote, 1909, described from <a href="/wiki/Faiyum" title="Faiyum">Faiyum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, is found along the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> north of <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Halfa" title="Wadi Halfa">Wadi Halfa</a>, Sudan. It intergrades with <i>bibilicus</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Sinai" title="Sinai" class="mw-redirect">Sinai</a>, and with <i>rufidorsalis</i> in a narrow zone around Wadi Halfa. It has been recorded in <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Praed_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Praed-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. persicus</i> <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Zarudny" title="Nikolai Zarudny">Zarudny</a> and Kudashev, 1916, described from the <a href="/wiki/Karun_River" title="Karun River" class="mw-redirect">Karun River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Khuzestan" title="Khuzestan" class="mw-redirect">Khuzestan</a>, Iran, is found in the western and central Iran south of the <a href="/wiki/Alborz" title="Alborz">Alborz</a> mountains, intergrading with <i>indicus</i> in eastern Iran, and Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. biblicus</i> <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Hartert" title="Ernst Hartert">Hartert</a>, 1910, described from <a href="/wiki/Palestine" title="Palestine">Palestine</a>, is found in the Middle East from <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and southeastern Turkey to the Sinai in the west and from <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt><i>indicus</i> group</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>P. d. hyrcanus</i> Zarudny and Kudashev, 1916, described from <a href="/wiki/Gorgan" title="Gorgan">Gorgan</a>, Iran, is found along the southern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> from Gorgan to southeastern Azerbaijan. It intergrades with <i>persicus</i> in the Alborz mountains, and with <i>bibilicus</i> to the west. It is the subspecies with the smallest range.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. bactrianus</i> Zarudny and Kudashev, 1916, described from <a href="/wiki/Tashkent" title="Tashkent">Tashkent</a>, is found in southern Kazakhstan to the <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a> and northern Iran and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>. It intergrades with <i>persicus</i> in <a href="/wiki/Baluchistan" title="Baluchistan" class="mw-redirect">Baluchistan</a> and with <i>indicus</i> across central Afghanistan. Unlike most other House Sparrow subspecies, it is almost entirely migratory, wintering in the plains of the northern Indian subcontinent. It is found in open country rather than in settlements, which are occupied by the Eurasian Tree Sparrow in its range.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> There is an exceptional record from Sudan.<sup id="cite_ref-Praed_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Praed-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. parkini</i> <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Whistler" title="Hugh Whistler">Whistler</a>, 1920, described from <a href="/wiki/Srinagar" title="Srinagar">Srinagar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>, is found in the western <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a> to southeastern <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>. It is migratory, like <i>bactrianus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. indicus</i> <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Jardine,_7th_Baronet" title="Sir William Jardine, 7th Baronet">Jardine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prideaux_John_Selby" title="Prideaux John Selby">Selby</a>, 1831, described from <a href="/wiki/Bangalore" title="Bangalore">Bangalore</a>, is found in the Indian subcontinent south of the Himalayas, in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, eastern Iran, and southwestern Arabia as far as southern Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. hufufae</i> <a href="/wiki/Claud_Buchanan_Ticehurst" title="Claud Buchanan Ticehurst">Ticehurst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Frederic_Cheeseman" title="Thomas Frederic Cheeseman">Cheeseman</a>, 1924, described from <a href="/wiki/Hofuf" title="Hofuf">Hofuf</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, is found in northeastern Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>P. d. rufidorsalis</i> <a href="/wiki/C._L._Brehm" title="C. L. Brehm" class="mw-redirect">C. L. Brehm</a>, 1855, described from <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, is found in the Nile valley from Wadi Halfa south to <a href="/wiki/Renk,_South_Sudan" title="Renk, South Sudan">Renk</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Summers126.E2.80.93128_61-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers126.E2.80.93128-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Praed_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Praed-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> and in eastern Sudan, northern Ethiopia to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> coast in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> It has also been introduced to <a href="/wiki/Moh%C3%A9li" title="Mohéli">Mohéli</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG/170px-House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG/255px-House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG/340px-House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow_nesting_in_saguaro_cactus.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
By a nest in a <a href="/wiki/Saguaro" title="Saguaro">saguaro</a> cactus in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook,_New_Zealand.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/220px-Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/330px-Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/440px-Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook%2C_New_Zealand.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-Mount_Cook,_New_Zealand.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
House Sparrows perching on a roof, during winter in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Alps" title="Southern Alps">Southern Alps</a> of New Zealand</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The House Sparrow originated in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and spread, along with agriculture, to most of Eurasia and parts of North Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Since the mid-nineteenth century, it has reached most of the world, due chiefly to deliberate introductions, but also through natural and shipborne dispersal.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Its introduced range encompasses most of North America, <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>, southern South America, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern Africa</a>, part of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, and islands throughout the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson5_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson5-68"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> It has greatly extended its range in northern Eurasia since the 1850s,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> and continues to do so, as was shown by the colonisations around 1990 of <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rishiri_Island" title="Rishiri Island">Rishiri Island</a>, Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> The extent of its range makes it the most widely distributed wild bird on the planet.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson5_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson5-68"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Introductions">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Introductions">Introductions</span></h3>
<p>The House Sparrow has become highly successful in most parts of the world where it has been introduced. This is mostly due to its early adaptation to living with humans, and its adaptability to a wide range of conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Other factors may include its robust immune response, compared to the Eurasian Tree Sparrow.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Where introduced, it can extend its range quickly, sometimes at a rate of over 230 kilometres (140&#160;mi) per year.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> In many parts of the world it has been characterized as a pest, and poses a threat to native birds.<sup id="cite_ref-scapegoat_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scapegoat-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> A few introductions have died out or been of limited success, such as those to <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verde</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first of many successful introductions to North America occurred when birds from England were released in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, in 1852.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Healy_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Healy-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> The House Sparrow now occurs from the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a> to southern <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> and it is one of the most abundant birds in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-scapegoat_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scapegoat-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> The House Sparrow was first introduced to Australia in 1863 at <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> and is common throughout eastern part of the continent,<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> but has been prevented from establishing itself in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>, where every House Sparrow found in the state is killed.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> House Sparrows were introduced in New Zealand in 1859, and from there reached many of the Pacific islands, including <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson25_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson25-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In southern Africa birds of both the European subspecies <i>domesticus</i> and the Indian subspecies <i>indicus</i> were introduced around 1900. Birds of <i>domesticus</i> ancestry are confined to a few towns, while <i>indicus</i> birds have spread rapidly, reaching <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> in the 1980s. Despite this success, native relatives such as the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Sparrow" title="Cape Sparrow">Cape Sparrow</a> also occur in towns, competing successfully with it.<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> In South America, it was first introduced near <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a> around 1870, and quickly became common in most of the southern part of the continent. It now occurs almost continuously from <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> to the fringes of <a href="/wiki/Amazonia" title="Amazonia" class="mw-redirect">Amazonia</a>, with isolated populations as far north as coastal Venezuela.<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_77-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span></h3>
<p>The House Sparrow is closely associated with human habitation and cultivation.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersHabitat_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersHabitat-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> It is not an obligate <a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensal</a> of humans as some have suggested, as Central Asian birds usually breed away from humans in open country,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> and birds elsewhere are found away from humans.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersHabitat_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersHabitat-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> The only terrestrial habitats in which House Sparrows do not occur are dense forest and <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>. Well adapted to living around humans, it frequently lives and even breeds indoors, especially in factories, warehouses and zoos.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersHabitat_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersHabitat-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> It has been recorded breeding in an English <a href="/wiki/Coal_mine" title="Coal mine" class="mw-redirect">coal mine</a> 640&#160;m (2,100&#160;ft) below ground,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> and feeding on the <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a>'s observation deck at night.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> It reaches its greatest densities in urban centres, but its reproductive success is greater in suburbs, where insects are more abundant.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersHabitat_85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersHabitat-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Poel_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poel-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> On a larger scale, it is most abundant in wheat-growing areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwestern United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>It tolerates a variety of climates, but prefers drier conditions, especially in moist tropical climates.<sup id="cite_ref-dist-changes_77-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dist-changes-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SummersHabitat_85-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersHabitat-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> It has several adaptations to dry areas, including a high salt tolerance<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> and an ability to survive without water by ingesting <a href="/wiki/Berry" title="Berry">berries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Walsberg_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walsberg-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> In most of eastern Asia the House Sparrow is entirely absent, replaced by the Eurasian Tree Sparrow.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Where these two species overlap, the House Sparrow is usually more common than the Eurasian Tree Sparrow, but one species may replace the other in a manner that <a href="/wiki/Maud_Doria_Haviland" title="Maud Doria Haviland">Maud Doria Haviland</a> described as "random, or even capricious".<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> In most of its range the House Sparrow is extremely common, despite some declines,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> but in marginal habitats such as rainforest or mountain ranges, its distribution can be spotty.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersHabitat_85-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersHabitat-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright" style="width: 366px;">
<div class="thumbinner">
<div style="float: left; margin: 1px; width: 192px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-England_-bathing-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Passer_domesticus_-England_-bathing-8.jpg/190px-Passer_domesticus_-England_-bathing-8.jpg" width="190" height="126" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Passer_domesticus_-England_-bathing-8.jpg/285px-Passer_domesticus_-England_-bathing-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Passer_domesticus_-England_-bathing-8.jpg/380px-Passer_domesticus_-England_-bathing-8.jpg 2x" /></a></div>
</div>
<div style="float: left; margin: 1px; width: 162px">
<div class="thumbimage">
<div id="mwe_player_2" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:160px;height:120px" data-videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;position:relative;display:block;width:640px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_3&quot; style=&quot;width:640px;height:480px&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/House_Sparrow_dust_bath.ogv/mid-House_Sparrow_dust_bath.ogv.jpg&quot; controls=&quot;&quot; preload=&quot;none&quot; class=&quot;kskin&quot; data-durationhint=&quot;25.066666666667&quot; data-startoffset=&quot;0&quot; data-mwtitle=&quot;House_Sparrow_dust_bath.ogv&quot; data-mwprovider=&quot;wikimediacommons&quot;&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/House_Sparrow_dust_bath.ogv&quot; data-title=&quot;Original ogg, 640 × 480 (3.5Mbps)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;Ogg source&quot; data-width=&quot;640&quot; data-height=&quot;480&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;3504375&quot; data-framerate=&quot;30&quot; /&gt;Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.&lt;br /&gt;
