</div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear: left; text-align: left; background: transparent">The House Sparrow often bathes in water (at left) or in dust (at right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The House Sparrow is a very social bird. It is gregarious at all seasons when feeding, often forming flocks with other types of bird.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> It also roosts communally, its nests are usually grouped together in clumps, and it engages in social activities such as <a href="/wiki/Dust_bath" title="Dust bath">dust</a> and water bathing, and "social singing", in which birds call together in bushes.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersBehaviour_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersBehaviour-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> The House Sparrow feeds mostly on the ground, but it flocks in trees and bushes.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersBehaviour_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersBehaviour-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> Non-breeding sparrows roost in large groups in trees, gathering beforehand and calling together.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersBehaviour_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersBehaviour-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> At feeding stations and nests, female House Sparrows are dominant despite their smaller size.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Most birds do not move more than a few kilometres. However, there is limited migration in all regions. Some young birds disperse long distances, especially on coasts, and mountain birds move to lower altitudes in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersBehaviour_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersBehaviour-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Two subspecies, <i>bactrianus</i> and <i>parkini</i>, are predominately <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a>. Unlike the birds in sedentary populations that migrate, birds of migratory subspecies prepare for migration by putting on weight.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersBehaviour_99-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersBehaviour-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px"><video id="mwe_player_4" style="width:220px;height:165px" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Passer_domesticus_female_-_foraging_plants.ogg/mid-Passer_domesticus_female_-_foraging_plants.ogg.jpg" controls="" preload="none" class="kskin" data-durationhint="40.006673340007" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Passer_domesticus_female_-_foraging_plants.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Passer_domesticus_female_-_foraging_plants.ogg" data-title="Original ogg, 640 × 480 (788kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="640" data-height="480" data-bandwidth="788098" data-framerate="29.97"></source>Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.<br />
You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Passer_domesticus_female_-_foraging_plants.ogg">download the clip</a> or <a href="http://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">download a player</a> to play the clip in your browser.</video></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_female_-_foraging_plants.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Female foraging in Germany</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As an adult, the House Sparrow mostly feeds on the seeds of <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weeds</a>, but it is opportunistic and adaptable, and eats whatever foods are available.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson273.E2.80.93275_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson273.E2.80.93275-105"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> It can perform complex and unusual tasks to obtain food, such as opening automatic doors to enter supermarkets,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> clinging to hotel walls to watch vacationers on their balconies,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nectar_robbing" title="Nectar robbing">nectar robbing</a> <a href="/wiki/Kowhai" title="Kowhai" class="mw-redirect">kowhai</a> flowers.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> In common with many other birds, the House Sparrow requires <a href="/wiki/Gastrolith" title="Gastrolith">grit</a> to digest the hard seeds it eats. Grit can be either stone, often grains of masonry, or the shells of eggs or snails; oblong and rough grains are preferred.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Several studies of the House Sparrow in temperate agricultural areas have found the proportion of seeds in its diet to be about 90%.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson273.E2.80.93275_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson273.E2.80.93275-105"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SummersFood_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersFood-112"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> It will eat almost any seeds, but where it has a choice, it prefers <a href="/wiki/Oat" title="Oat">oats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> In urban areas, the House Sparrow feeds largely on food provided directly or indirectly by humans, such as bread, though it prefers raw seeds.<sup id="cite_ref-SummersFood_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersFood-112"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> The House Sparrow also eats some plant matter besides seeds, including <a href="/wiki/Bud" title="Bud">buds</a>, berries, and fruits such as grapes and cherries.<sup id="cite_ref-Walsberg_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walsberg-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SummersFood_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SummersFood-112"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> In temperate areas, the House Sparrow has an unusual habit of tearing flowers, especially yellow ones, in the spring.<sup id="cite_ref-NN35.2C_38.E2.80.9339_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NN35.2C_38.E2.80.9339-115"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Animals form another important part of the House Sparrow's diet, chiefly <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, of which <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">caterpillars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diptera" title="Diptera" class="mw-redirect">dipteran</a> flies, and <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a> are especially important. Various non-insect <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> are eaten, as are <a href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc" class="mw-redirect">molluscs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> where available, <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson273.E2.80.93275_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson273.E2.80.93275-105"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Young House Sparrows are fed mostly on insects until about fifteen days after hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> They are also given small quantities of seeds, spiders, and grit. In most places, <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cricket_(insect)" title="Cricket (insect)">crickets</a> are most the abundant foods of nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-AndersonNestlingDiet_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonNestlingDiet-117"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hemiptera" title="Hemiptera">True bugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sawfly" title="Sawfly">sawflies</a>, and beetles are also important, but House Sparrows will take advantage of whatever foods are abundant to feed their young.<sup id="cite_ref-AndersonNestlingDiet_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonNestlingDiet-117"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ivanov_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanov-119"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> House Sparrows have been observed <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">stealing</a> prey from other birds, including <a href="/wiki/American_Robin" title="American Robin">American Robins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg/220px-House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg/330px-House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg/440px-House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrows_mating_I_IMG_0066.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A pair of the subspecies <i>indicus</i> mating in <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>House Sparrows can breed in the breeding season immediately following their hatching, and sometimes attempt to do so. Some birds breeding for the first time in tropical areas are only a few months old and still have juvenile plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> Birds breeding for the first time are rarely successful in raising young, and reproductive success increases with age, as older birds breed earlier in the breeding season, and fledge more young.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> As the breeding season approaches, hormone releases trigger enormous increases in the size of the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_organs" title="Sexual organs" class="mw-redirect">sexual organs</a> and changes in day length lead males to start calling by nesting sites.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> The timing of mating and egg-laying varies geographically, and between specific locations and years. This is because a sufficient supply of insects is needed for egg formation and feeding nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers144.E2.80.93147_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers144.E2.80.93147-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Males take up nesting sites before the breeding season, by frequently calling beside them. Unmated males start nest construction and call particularly frequently to attract females. When a female approaches a male during this period, the male <a href="/wiki/Courtship_display" title="Courtship display">displays</a> by moving up and down while drooping and shivering his wings, pushing up his head, raising and spreading his tail, and showing his bib.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers144.E2.80.93147_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers144.E2.80.93147-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Males may try to mate with females while calling or displaying. In response, a female will adopt a threatening posture and attack a male before flying away, pursued by the male. The male displays in front of her, attracting other males, who also pursue and display to the female. This group display usually does not immediately result in copulations.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers144.E2.80.93147_124-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers144.E2.80.93147-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Other males usually do not copulate with the female.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers142.E2.80.93143_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers142.E2.80.93143-125"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> Copulation is typically initiated by the female giving a soft <i>dee-dee-dee</i> call to the male. Birds of a pair <a href="/wiki/Copulate" title="Copulate" class="mw-redirect">copulate</a> frequently until the female is laying eggs, and the male mounts the female repeatedly each time a pair mates.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers144.E2.80.93147_124-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers144.E2.80.93147-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The House Sparrow is <a href="/wiki/Monogamous" title="Monogamous" class="mw-redirect">monogamous</a>, and typically mates for life. Birds from pairs often engage in extra-pair copulations, so about 15% of House Sparrow fledglings are unrelated to their mother's mate.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> Male House Sparrows guard their mates carefully to avoid being cuckolded, and most extra-pair copulation occurs away from nest sites.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers142.E2.80.93143_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers142.E2.80.93143-125"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Males may sometimes have multiple mates, and bigamy is mostly limited by aggression between females.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Many birds do not find a nest and a mate, and instead may serve as <a href="/wiki/Helpers_at_the_nest" title="Helpers at the nest">helpers</a> around the nest for mated pairs, a role which increases the chances of being chosen to replace a lost mate. Lost mates of both sexes can be replaced quickly during the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers142.E2.80.93143_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers142.E2.80.93143-125"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> The formation of a pair and the bond between the two birds is tied to the holding of a nest site, though paired House Sparrows can recognise each other away from the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers144.E2.80.93147_124-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers144.E2.80.93147-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nesting">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Nesting">Nesting</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg/220px-Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg/330px-Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg/440px-Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-with_an_insect_for_her_young_-female_-nest-8b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Female bringing food for young in a nest made in a tree hole in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Nest sites are varied, though cavities are preferred. Nests are most frequently built in the <a href="/wiki/Eaves" title="Eaves">eaves</a> and other crevices of houses. Holes in cliffs and banks, or in <a href="/wiki/Tree_hollow" title="Tree hollow">tree hollows</a> are also used.<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Indykiewicz_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indykiewicz-132"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> It sometimes excavates its own nests in sandy banks or rotten branches, but more frequently uses the nests of other birds such as those of <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallows</a> in banks and cliffs, and old tree cavity nests.<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> It usually uses deserted nests, though sometimes it usurps active ones.<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gowaty_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gowaty-133"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> Tree hollows are more commonly used in North America than in Europe,<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> putting the sparrows in competition with <a href="/wiki/Bluebird" title="Bluebird">bluebirds</a> and other North American cavity nesters, and thereby contributing to their population declines.<sup id="cite_ref-scapegoat_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scapegoat-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Especially in warmer areas, the House Sparrow may build its nests in the open, on the branches of trees, especially evergreens and hawthorns, or in the nests of large birds such as <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">storks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magpie" title="Magpie">magpies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers144.E2.80.93147_124-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers144.E2.80.93147-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> In open nesting sites, breeding success tends to be lower, since breeding begins late and the nest can easily be destroyed or damaged by storms.<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Less common nesting sites include <a href="/wiki/Street_light" title="Street light">street lights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neon_sign" title="Neon sign">neon signs</a>, favoured for their warmth; and the old open-topped nests of other songbirds, which are then domed over.<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Indykiewicz_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indykiewicz-132"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The nest is usually domed, though it may lack a roof in enclosed sites.<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> It has an outer layer of stems and roots, a middle layer of dead grass and leaves, and a lining of feathers, as well as of paper and other soft materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Indykiewicz_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indykiewicz-132"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> Nests typically have external dimensions of 20 × 30&#160;cm (8 × 12&#160;in),<sup id="cite_ref-Summers144.E2.80.93147_124-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers144.E2.80.93147-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> but their size varies greatly.<sup id="cite_ref-Indykiewicz_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indykiewicz-132"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> The building of the nest is initiated by the unmated male while displaying to females. The female assists in building, but is less active than the male.<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Some nest building occurs throughout the year, especially after moult in autumn. In colder areas House Sparrows build specially created roost nests, or roost in street lights, to avoid losing heat during the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> House Sparrows do not hold territories, but they defend their nests aggressively against intruders of the same sex.<sup id="cite_ref-NNnest_131-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNnest-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>House Sparrows' nests support a wide range of scavenging insects, including nest flies such as <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neottiophilum_praestum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Neottiophilum praestum (page does not exist)">Neottiophilum praestum</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Protocalliphora" title="Protocalliphora">Protocalliphora</a></i> blowflies,<sup id="cite_ref-NN131.E2.80.93132_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NN131.E2.80.93132-137"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> and over 1,400 species of beetle.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Eggs and young">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs_and_young">Eggs and young</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG/220px-Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG/330px-Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG/440px-Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passerdomesticuseggs.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Eggs in a nest</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">Clutches</a> usually comprise four or five <a href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a>, though numbers from one to ten have been recorded. At least two clutches are usually laid, and up to seven a year may be laid in the tropics or four a year in temperate latitudes. When fewer clutches are laid in a year, especially at higher latitudes, the number of eggs per clutch is greater. Central Asian House Sparrows, which migrate and have only one clutch a year, average 6.5&#160;eggs in a clutch. Clutch size is also affected by environmental and seasonal conditions, female age, and breeding density.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers148.E2.80.93149_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers148.E2.80.93149-140"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some intraspecific <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasitism</a> occurs, and instances of unusually large numbers of eggs in a nest may be the result of females laying eggs in the nests of their neighbours. Such foreign eggs are sometimes recognised and ejected by females.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers148.E2.80.93149_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers148.E2.80.93149-140"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anderson145.E2.80.93146_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson145.E2.80.93146-142"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> The House Sparrow is a victim of interspecific brood parasites, but only rarely, since it usually uses nests in holes too small for parasites to enter, and it feeds its young foods unsuitable for young parasites.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> In turn, the House Sparrow has once been recorded as a brood parasite of the <a href="/wiki/American_Cliff_Swallow" title="American Cliff Swallow">American Cliff Swallow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson145.E2.80.93146_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson145.E2.80.93146-142"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The eggs are white, bluish-white, or greenish-white, spotted with brown or grey.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-146"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> Subelliptical in shape,<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> they range from 20–22 mm (0.79–0.87 in) in length and 14–16 mm (0.55–0.63 in) in width,<sup id="cite_ref-all_about_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-all_about-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> have an average mass of 2.9&#160;g (0.10&#160;oz),<sup id="cite_ref-BTO_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BTO-147"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> and an average surface area of 9.18&#160;cm<sup>2</sup> (1.423&#160;sq&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Paganelli_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paganelli-148"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> Eggs from the tropical subspecies are distinctly smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-BM_eggs_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BM_eggs-149"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> Eggs begin to develop with the deposition of <a href="/wiki/Egg_yolk" title="Egg yolk">yolk</a> in the ovary a few days before ovulation. In the day between ovulation and laying, <a href="/wiki/Egg_white" title="Egg white">egg white</a> forms, followed by <a href="/wiki/Eggshell" title="Eggshell">eggshell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Eggs laid later in a clutch are larger, as are those laid by larger females, and egg size is hereditary. Eggs decrease slightly in size from laying to hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> The yolk comprises 25% of the egg, the egg white 68%, and the shell 7%. Eggs are watery, being 79% liquid, and otherwise mostly protein.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_chick.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Naked and blind chick" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/House_sparrow_chick.jpg/220px-House_sparrow_chick.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/House_sparrow_chick.jpg/330px-House_sparrow_chick.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/House_sparrow_chick.jpg/440px-House_sparrow_chick.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_chick.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Hatchling</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The female develops a <a href="/wiki/Brood_patch" title="Brood patch">brood patch</a> of bare skin and plays the main part in <a href="/wiki/Avian_incubation" title="Avian incubation" class="mw-redirect">incubating</a> the eggs. The male helps, but can only cover the eggs rather than truly incubating them. The female spends the night incubating during this period, while the male roosts near the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers148.E2.80.93149_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers148.E2.80.93149-140"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Eggs hatch at the same time, after a short incubation period lasting 11–14&#160;days, and exceptionally for as many as 17 or as few as 9.<sup id="cite_ref-Groschupf_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groschupf-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Summers144.E2.80.93147_124-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers144.E2.80.93147-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> The length of the incubation period decreases as ambient temperature increases later in the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers149.E2.80.93150_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers149.E2.80.93150-155"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_detail(loz).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg/220px-Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg/330px-Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg/440px-Passer_domesticus_detail%28loz%29.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_detail(loz).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A female feeding a fledgling</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Young House Sparrows typically remain in the nest for 11 to 23 days, normally 14 to 16 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-146"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Summers149.E2.80.93150_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers149.E2.80.93150-155"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mauser_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mauser-156"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> During this time, they are fed by both parents. As newly hatched House Sparrows do not have sufficient insulation they are brooded for a few days, or longer in cold conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Summers149.E2.80.93150_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers149.E2.80.93150-155"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HbvPddVerhalten2_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HbvPddVerhalten2-157"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> The parents swallow the droppings produced by the hatchlings during the first few days; later, the droppings are moved up to 20&#160;m (66&#160;ft) away from the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-HbvPddVerhalten2_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HbvPddVerhalten2-157"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The chicks' eyes open after about four days and, at an age of about eight days, the young birds get their first <a href="/wiki/Down_feather" title="Down feather">down</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Poole_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-146"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mauser_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mauser-156"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> If both parents perish, the ensuing intensive begging sounds of the young will often attract replacement parents who feed them until they can sustain themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-HbvPddVerhalten2_157-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HbvPddVerhalten2-157"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> All the young in the nest leave it during the same period of a few hours. At this stage they are normally able to fly. They start feeding themselves partly after one or two days, and sustain themselves completely after 7 to 10 days, 14 at the latest.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Survival">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Survival">Survival</span></h2>
<p>In adult House Sparrows, annual survival is 45-65%.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> After fledging and leaving the care of their parents, young sparrows have a high mortality rate, which lessens as they grow older and more experienced. Only about 20–25% of birds hatched survive to their first breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> The oldest known wild House Sparrow lived for nearly two decades; it was found dead 19&#160;years and 9&#160;months after it was <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringed</a> in Denmark.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> The oldest recorded captive House Sparrow lived for 23&#160;years.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> The typical ratio of males to females in a population is uncertain due to problems in collecting data, but a very slight preponderance of males at all ages is usual.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Predation">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Predation">Predation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cat-eating-prey.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cat-eating-prey.jpg/220px-Cat-eating-prey.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cat-eating-prey.jpg/330px-Cat-eating-prey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cat-eating-prey.jpg/440px-Cat-eating-prey.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cat-eating-prey.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A male being eaten by a cat. <a href="/wiki/Domestic_cat" title="Domestic cat" class="mw-redirect">Domestic cats</a> are one of the main predators of the House Sparrow.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The House Sparrow's main predators are <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">birds of prey</a>, but many other animals prey on them, including <a href="/wiki/Corvidae" title="Corvidae">corvids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrels</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson30.E2.80.9333_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson30.E2.80.9333-166"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> and even humans, as the House Sparrow has been consumed by humans in many parts of the world, and still is in parts of the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Most species of bird of prey have been recorded preying on the House Sparrow in places where there are extensive records. <a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">Accipiters</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">Merlin</a> in particular are major predators, though cats are likely to have a greater impact on House Sparrow populations.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson30.E2.80.9333_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson30.E2.80.9333-166"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> The House Sparrow is also a common victim of <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">roadkill</a>; on European roads, it is the bird most frequently found dead.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Parasites and disease">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Parasites_and_disease">Parasites and disease</span></h3>
<p>The House Sparrow is host to a huge number of parasites and diseases, and the effect of most is unknown. Ornithologist Ted R. Anderson listed thousands, noting that his list was incomplete.<sup id="cite_ref-AndersonDisease_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonDisease-169"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> The commonly recorded bacterial pathogens of the House Sparrow are often those common in humans, and include <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Salmonella</i> is common in the House Sparrow, and a comprehensive study of House Sparrow disease found it in 13% of sparrows tested. <i>Salmonella</i> epidemics in the spring and winter can kill large numbers of sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-AndersonDisease_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonDisease-169"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> The House Sparrow hosts <a href="/wiki/Avian_pox" title="Avian pox" class="mw-redirect">avian pox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avian_malaria" title="Avian malaria">avian malaria</a>, which it has spread to the native forest birds of Hawaii.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> Many of the diseases hosted by the House Sparrow are also present in humans and domestic animals, for which the House Sparrow acts as a <a href="/wiki/Natural_reservoir" title="Natural reservoir">reservoir host</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arbovirus_infection" title="Arbovirus infection" class="mw-redirect">Arboviruses</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">West Nile virus</a>, which most commonly infect insects and mammals, survive winters in temperate areas by going dormant in birds such as the House Sparrow.<sup id="cite_ref-AndersonDisease_169-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonDisease-169"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> There are a few records of disease extirpating House Sparrow populations, especially from Scottish islands, but this seems to be rare.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The House Sparrow is infested by a number of external parasites, which usually cause little harm to adult sparrows. In Europe, the most common <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mite</a> found on sparrows is <i><a href="/wiki/Proctophyllodes" title="Proctophyllodes">Proctophyllodes</a></i>, the most common <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a> are <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Argas_reflexus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Argas reflexus (page does not exist)">Argas reflexus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ixodes_arboricola" title="Ixodes arboricola">Ixodes arboricola</a></i>, and the most common flea on the House Sparrow is <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratophyllus_gallinae" title="Ceratophyllus gallinae">Ceratophyllus gallinae</a></i>. A number of <a href="/wiki/Chewing_louse" title="Chewing louse" class="mw-redirect">chewing lice</a> occupy different <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niches</a> on the House Sparrow's body. <i><a href="/wiki/Menacanthus" title="Menacanthus">Menacanthus</a></i> lice occur across the House Sparrow's body, where they feed on blood and feathers, while <i><a href="/wiki/Brueelia" title="Brueelia">Brueelia</a></i> lice feed on feathers and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Philopterus_fringillae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Philopterus fringillae (page does not exist)">Philopterus fringillae</a></i> occurs on the head.<sup id="cite_ref-NN131.E2.80.93132_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NN131.E2.80.93132-137"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Relationships with humans">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships_with_humans">Relationships with humans</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px"><video id="mwe_player_5" style="width:220px;height:165px" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogg/mid-Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogg.jpg" controls="" preload="none" class="kskin" data-durationhint="50.016683350017" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogg" data-title="Original ogg, 640 × 480 (688kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="640" data-height="480" data-bandwidth="688147" data-framerate="29.97"></source>Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.<br />
You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogg">download the clip</a> or <a href="http://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">download a player</a> to play the clip in your browser.</video></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_-_gathering_at_fluorescent_tube.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Flocking and chirping together beneath a <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_tube" title="Fluorescent tube" class="mw-redirect">fluorescent tube</a> light in Germany</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The House Sparrow is closely associated with humans. They are believed to have become associated with humans around 10,000 years ago. Subspecies <i>bactrianus</i> is least associated with humans and considered to be evolutionarily closer to the ancestral non-commensal populations.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> Usually, it is regarded as a pest, since it consumes agricultural products and spreads disease to humans and their domestic animals.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> Even <a href="/wiki/Birdwatching" title="Birdwatching">birdwatchers</a> often hold it in little regard because of its molestation of other birds.<sup id="cite_ref-scapegoat_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scapegoat-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> In most of the world the House Sparrow is not protected by law. Attempts to control House Sparrows include the trapping, poisoning, or shooting of adults; the destruction of their nests and eggs; or less directly, blocking nest holes and scaring off sparrows with noise, glue, or porcupine wire.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSG_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSG-177"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the House Sparrow can be beneficial to humans as well, especially by eating insect pests, and attempts at the large-scale control of the House Sparrow have failed.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The House Sparrow has long been used as a food item. From around 1560 to at least the 19th century in northern Europe, <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">earthenware</a> "sparrow pots" were hung from eaves to attract nesting birds so that the young could be readily harvested. Wild birds were trapped in nets in large numbers, and sparrow pie was a traditional dish, thought, because of the association of sparrows with lechery, to have <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">aphrodisiac</a> properties. Sparrows were also trapped as food for <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconer's</a> birds and zoo animals. In the early part of the twentieth century, sparrow clubs culled many millions of birds and eggs in an attempt to control numbers of this perceived pest, but with only a localised impact on numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>House Sparrows have been kept as pets at many times in history, though they have no bright plumage or attractive songs, and raising them is difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-OS.26M-pets_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OS.26M-pets-179"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span></h3>
<p>The House Sparrow has an extremely large range and population, and is not seriously threatened by human activities, so it is assessed as <a href="/wiki/Least_Concern" title="Least Concern">Least Concern</a> for conservation on the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN2012_0-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN2012-0"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> However, populations have been declining in many parts of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> These declines were first noticed in North America, where they were initially attributed to the spread of the <a href="/wiki/House_Finch" title="House Finch">House Finch</a>, but have been most severe in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup> Declines have not been universal, as no serious declines have been reported from <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, but have even occurred in Australia, where the House Sparrow was introduced recently.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, populations peaked in the early 1970s,<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> but have since declined by 68% overall,<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> and about 90% in some regions.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy-188"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> In London, the House Sparrow almost disappeared from the central city.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy-188"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> The numbers of House Sparrows in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> have dropped in half since the 1980s,<sup id="cite_ref-Poel_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poel-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> so the House Sparrow is even considered an <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> This status which came to widespread attention after a female House Sparrow, referred to as the "<i><a href="/wiki/Domino_Day_2005_sparrow" title="Domino Day 2005 sparrow">Dominomus</a></i>", was killed after knocking down dominoes arranged as part of an attempt to set a world record.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> These declines are not unprecedented, as similar reductions in population occurred when the internal combustion engine replaced horses in the 1920s and a major source of food in the form of grain spillage was lost.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Various causes for the dramatic decreases in population have been proposed, including predation, in particular by <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian Sparrowhawk">Eurasian Sparrowhawks</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy3_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy3-196"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> electromagnetic radiation from mobile phones;<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> and diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy2_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy2-198"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> A shortage of nesting sites caused by changes in urban building design is probably a factor, and conservation organisations have encouraged the use of special nest boxes for sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy2_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy2-198"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> A primary cause of the decline seems to be an insufficient supply of insect food for nestling sparrows.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy2_198-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy2-198"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup> Declines in insect populations result from an increase of monoculture crops, the heavy use of pesticides,<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> the replacement of native plants in cities with introduced plants and parking areas,<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> and possibly the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Unleaded_petrol" title="Unleaded petrol" class="mw-redirect">unleaded petrol</a>, which produces toxic compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Methyl_nitrite" title="Methyl nitrite">methyl nitrite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Protecting insect habitats on farms,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> and planting native plants in cities benefit the House Sparrow, as does establishing urban green spaces.<sup id="cite_ref-Leylandii_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leylandii-211"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> To raise awareness of threats to the House Sparrow, <a href="/wiki/World_Sparrow_Day" title="World Sparrow Day">World Sparrow Day</a> has been celebrated on 20 March across the world since 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=House_Sparrow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cultural associations">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_associations">Cultural associations</span></h3>
<p>To many people across the world, the House Sparrow is the most familiar wild animal and, because of its association with humans and familiarity, it is frequently used to represent the common and vulgar, or the lewd.<sup id="cite_ref-NN_49.2C_215_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NN_49.2C_215-214"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> One of the reasons for the introduction of House Sparrows throughout the world was their association with the European homeland of many immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-Healy_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Healy-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Birds usually described later as sparrows are referred to in many works of ancient literature and religious texts in Europe and western Asia. These references may not always refer specifically to the House Sparrow, or even to small, seed-eating birds, but later writers who were inspired by these texts often had the House Sparrow in mind.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NN_49.2C_215_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NN_49.2C_215-214"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> In particular, sparrows were associated by the ancient Greeks with <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a>, the goddess of love, due to their perceived lustfulness, an association echoed by later writers such as <a href="/wiki/Chaucer" title="Chaucer" class="mw-redirect">Chaucer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare" class="mw-redirect">Shakespeare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_38-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OS.26M-pets_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OS.26M-pets-179"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NN_49.2C_215_214-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NN_49.2C_215-214"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> Jesus's use of "sparrows" as an example of divine providence in the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> also inspired later references, such as that in Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-NN_49.2C_215_214-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NN_49.2C_215-214"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_music" title="Gospel music">Gospel</a> <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymn</a> <i><a href="/wiki/His_Eye_Is_on_the_Sparrow" title="His Eye Is on the Sparrow">His Eye Is on the Sparrow</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="float:right">
<table class='mw-hiero-table mw-hiero-outer' dir='ltr'>
<tr>
<td>
<table class="mw-hiero-table">
<tr>
<td><img style='margin: 1px; height: 28px;' src='//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_G37.png' title='G37' alt='G37' /></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<div style="display:inline;">The House Sparrow is only represented in ancient Egyptian art very rarely, but an <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyph" title="Egyptian hieroglyph" class="mw-redirect">Egyptian hieroglyph</a> is based on it. The sparrow hieroglyph had no phonetic value and was used as a determinative in words to indicate <i>small</i>, <i>narrow</i>, or <i>bad</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> An alternative view is that the hieroglyph meant "a prolific man" or "the revolution of a year".<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
